   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Web blocking in the United Kingdom</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
      Jump to: 
     <a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 
     <a href="#p-search">search</a> 
    </div> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Internet censorship in the United Kingdom">Internet censorship in the United Kingdom</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The precise number of <b>websites blocked in the United Kingdom</b> is unknown. Blocking techniques vary from one <a href="/wiki/Internet_Service_Provider" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet Service Provider">Internet Service Provider</a> (ISP) to another with some sites or specific URLs blocked by some ISPs and not others. Websites and services are blocked using a combination of data feeds from private <a href="/wiki/Content-control" class="mw-redirect" title="Content-control">content-control</a> technology companies, government agencies, NGOs, court orders in conjunction with the service administrators who may or may not have the power to unblock, additionally block, appeal or recategorise blocked content.</p> 
      <p></p>

      <p></p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>There are a number of different web blocking programmes in the UK. The high-profile default ISP filters and IWF filters have been referred to as a "pornwall",<sup id="cite_ref-whitelist_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitelist-1">[1]</a></sup> "porn filter",<sup id="cite_ref-gac_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gac-2">[2]</a></sup> "Hadrian's Firewall",<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> "Great Firewall of Britain"<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and the "Great Firewall of Cameron".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> However the programmes are usually referred to interchangeable or individually rather than collectively.</p> 
      <table class="wikitable sortable">

         <td><a href="/wiki/Internet_Watch_Foundation" title="Internet Watch Foundation">Internet Watch Foundation</a></td>

         <td>98.6% as of 2009<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></td> 
        </tr>

         <td>Copyright infringement site blocking</td>

         <td><a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">Rights</a> holder organisations court orders</td>

       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Incitement_to_ethnic_or_racial_hatred" title="Incitement to ethnic or racial hatred">Inciting racial hatred</a> was removed from the IWF's remit on the setting up of a police website for the purpose in April 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>The technical measures used to block sites include <a href="/wiki/DNS_hijacking" title="DNS hijacking">DNS hijacking</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNS_blocking" title="DNS blocking">DNS blocking</a>, <a href="/wiki/IP_address_blocking" title="IP address blocking">IP address blocking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Deep_packet_inspection" title="Deep packet inspection">Deep packet inspection</a>, making consistent verification problematic. One known method is ISP <a href="/wiki/Data_scraping" title="Data scraping">scraping</a> DNS of domains subject to blocking orders to produce a list of IPs to block.<sup id="cite_ref-yiffytechnical_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yiffytechnical-20">[20]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Open_Rights_Group" title="Open Rights Group">Open Rights Group</a> has proposed adding the new <a href="/wiki/HTTP_status_code" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP status code">HTTP status code</a> '451' to help streamline and add transparency to the process of determining when a site is blocked.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Active_programmes">Active programmes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Active programmes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Copyright">Copyright</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Copyright">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Court-ordered_blocks">Court-ordered blocks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Court-ordered blocks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>It is an established procedure in the UK for rights-holders to use 'Section 97'<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> court orders to require ISPs to block copyright-infringing sites.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> For instance, court orders obtained by the BPI in October 2013 resulted in the blocking of 21 file-sharing sites including <a href="/wiki/FilesTube" title="FilesTube">FilesTube</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torrentz" title="Torrentz">Torrentz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> There is a private agreement in principle between leading ISPs and rights holders, made with encouragement from government, to quickly restrict access to websites when presented with court orders.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The court orders are not made public<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and "overblocking" is sometimes reported, such as the accidental blocking of the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Times" title="Radio Times">Radio Times</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_F.C." title="Crystal Palace F.C.">Crystal Palace F.C.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Swift" title="Taylor Swift">Taylor Swift</a> and over 100 others websites in August 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>The practice originated as a result of a court order applied against an incidence of copyright infringement was that taken out by the <a href="/wiki/Motion_Picture_Association" title="Motion Picture Association">Motion Picture Association</a> in December 2010 at the request of <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_studio" class="mw-redirect" title="Hollywood studio">Hollywood studios</a>. The Association applied for an <a href="/wiki/Injunction" title="Injunction">injunction</a> to block access to <a href="/wiki/NewzBin" class="mw-redirect" title="NewzBin">NewzBin</a>2, a site which provided a search service for <a href="/wiki/UseNet" class="mw-redirect" title="UseNet">UseNet</a> content, indexing downloads of copyrighted content including movies and other material shared without permission. The application was lodged against BT, the largest <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">Internet service provider</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> with around six million customers. It required BT to use <a href="/wiki/Cleanfeed_(content_blocking_system)" title="Cleanfeed (content blocking system)">Cleanfeed</a> to block its customers' access to the site.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCcopyright_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCcopyright-30">[30]</a></sup> In July 2011 the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court of Justice</a> granted the injunction<sup id="cite_ref-bailii_2011_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bailii_2011-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and in October 2011 BT was ordered to block access to the website within fourteen days,<sup id="cite_ref-btdeadline_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btdeadline-33">[33]</a></sup> the first ruling of its kind under <a href="/wiki/Copyright_law_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Copyright law of the United Kingdom">UK copyright law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-block_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-block-34">[34]</a></sup> The precedent set was described by the Open Rights Group as "dangerous".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> BT did not appeal against the ruling and put the required block in place on 2 November 2011. Subsequent attempts to access the site from a BT <a href="/wiki/IP_address" title="IP address">IP address</a> were met with the message "Error - site blocked".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Newzbin released <a href="/wiki/Client_(computing)" title="Client (computing)">client software</a> to circumvent the BT blocking,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> using <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encryption</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tor_(anonymity_network)" title="Tor (anonymity network)">Tor</a> network.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Newzbin claimed that over 90% of its active UK users had downloaded its workaround software making the BT block ineffective. However, further court orders resulted in <a href="/wiki/Sky_Broadband" title="Sky Broadband">Sky</a> blocking access to Newzbin in December 2011<sup id="cite_ref-sky_block_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sky_block-39">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Media" title="Virgin Media">Virgin Media</a> blocking access to the site in August 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> On 28 November 2012 Newzbin announced the closure of its indexing service.</p> 
      <p>Meanwhile, in May 2012 the High Court ordered the blocking of <a href="/wiki/The_Pirate_Bay" title="The Pirate Bay">The Pirate Bay</a> by UK ISPs to prevent further copyright infringing movie and music downloads from the website.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The blocks were said to be quickly bypassed and a spokesman for <a href="/wiki/Pirate_Party_UK" title="Pirate Party UK">The Pirate Party</a> said public interest in the service following the ban had boosted traffic to the party's website.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In December 2012, the <a href="/wiki/British_Phonographic_Industry" title="British Phonographic Industry">British Phonographic Industry</a> (BPI) threatened legal action<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> against The Pirate Party after the party refused demands sent at the end of November to remove their <a href="/wiki/Proxy_server" title="Proxy server">proxy</a> to The Pirate Bay.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>In September 2013 an <a href="/wiki/Ofcom" title="Ofcom">Ofcom</a> survey revealed that 2% of Internet users are responsible for 74% of all copyright-infringing downloads in the UK, and that 29% of all downloads are of content which violates copyright.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>In October 2014 the first blocking order against trademark infringing consumer goods was passed against the major UK ISPs by <a href="/wiki/Richemont" title="Richemont">Richemont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cartier_(jeweler)" title="Cartier (jeweler)">Cartier International</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montblanc_(company)" title="Montblanc (company)">Montblanc</a> to block several domains.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: 
       <a href="/wiki/Websites_blocked_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Websites blocked in the United Kingdom">Websites blocked in the United Kingdom</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ISP_Default_network_blocking">ISP Default network blocking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: ISP Default network blocking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Internet customers in the UK are prohibited from accessing a range of web sites by default, because they have their Internet access filtered by their ISPs. The filtering program has applied to new ISP customers since the end of 2013, and has been extended to existing users on a rolling basis. A voluntary code of practice agreed by all four major ISPs<sup id="cite_ref-gov.uk_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gov.uk-48">[48]</a></sup> means that customers have to 'opt out' of the ISP filtering to gain access to the blocked content.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> However, the complex nature of the active monitoring systems means that users cannot usually opt out of the monitoring and re-routing of their data traffic, something which may render their data security vulnerable. The range of content blocked by ISPs can be varied over time.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>The idea for default filtering originated from manifesto commitments concerning "the commercialisation and sexualisation of childhood" given by the parties forming the <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron–Clegg coalition">Cameron–Clegg coalition</a> government in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> This was followed by a review (the <a href="/wiki/Bailey_Review" title="Bailey Review">Bailey Review</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and a consultation by the <a href="/wiki/UK_Council_for_Child_Internet_Safety" title="UK Council for Child Internet Safety">UK Council for Child Internet Safety</a> (UKCCIS).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Campaigning by <a href="/wiki/Claire_Perry" title="Claire Perry">Claire Perry</a> MP and the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a> newspaper resulted in significant public support for the idea of Internet filtering for the purposes of child protection.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> By 2013 there had already been considerable adoption of in-home filtering, with 43% of homes with children aged 5–15 having filters installed on their family computer.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Nevertheless, <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Minister">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> made it clear in July 2013 that his aim was to ensure that by the end of 2013 all ISPs would have a filtering system in place.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> As a result, three of the <i>Big 4</i> major ISPs (<a href="/wiki/TalkTalk_Group" title="TalkTalk Group">TalkTalk</a>, Sky and BT<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>) began applying default filtering to new customers in 2013<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> with the fourth major ISP, <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Media" title="Virgin Media">Virgin</a>, doing so in February 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Default filtering of existing customers was implemented by all four major ISPs during 2014 with the aim of ensuring that the system applied to 95% of all households by the end of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-Mail_Blocks_on_Internet_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mail_Blocks_on_Internet-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>TalkTalk already had content-control software available to comply with government requirements. Their <i>HomeSafe</i> internet filtering system was introduced in May 2011 as an opt-in product and was used for default filtering of new customers from March 2012. <i>HomeSafe</i> was praised by Cameron and is controlled and operated by the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Chinese</a> company <a href="/wiki/Huawei" title="Huawei">Huawei</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Huawei_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huawei-62">[62]</a></sup> After initial resistance<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> other ISPs had to commission new filtering systems to fulfil Government demands. Some smaller ISPs expressed their reluctance to take part in filtering, citing concerns over costs and civil liberties<sup id="cite_ref-aaisp_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aaisp-64">[64]</a></sup> but the government stated: "We expect the smaller ISPs to follow the lead being set by the larger providers".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Cameron said ISPs should choose their own preferred technical solution, but would be monitored to ensure filtering was done correctly. Nevertheless, the ISP <a href="/wiki/Andrews_and_Arnold" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrews and Arnold">Andrews and Arnold</a> does not censor any of its Internet connection all its broadband packages guarantee a 12-month notice should it start to censor any of its traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-aaisp_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aaisp-64">[64]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>In July 2014 Ofcom released a report into filter implementation and effectiveness across the fixed-line ISPs. At that point the <i>Big 4</i> major fixed-line ISPs comprised 93%<sup id="cite_ref-thinkbb2014_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thinkbb2014-8">[8]</a></sup> of the broadband market. They were all mandating filters be enabled as default for new customers, but overall take-up figures were low, with BT (5%), Sky (8%) and Virgin (4%). The figure was higher for TalkTalk (36%) as there had already been significant take-up of its system during the preceding three years.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The industry average was 13%.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In January 2015 Sky went further, blocking all material deemed unsuitable for children under the age of 13 for any of its five million customers who had not already opted out.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In the same month Talk Talk announced that customers who had not chosen whether to activate the company's filtering system would have to opt out if they wished it to be turned off.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In January 2016 Sky began sending all new and existing customers an email asking if they want to turn the filter on. Those customers who ignore the email have the filter turned on automatically.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_status">Legal status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Legal status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>The current legal status of ISP web blocking is voluntary, but there have been a number of attempts to introduce legislation to move it onto a mandatory footing. David Cameron first announced such legislation in July 2013<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> but default filtering was rejected at the September 2013 conference of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> (the Coalition Government's minor partner)<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and no Government legislation to this effect occurred during the 2010-15 Parliament.</p> 
      <p>Prior to the 2015 <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">United Kingdom general election, 2015</a> both the opposition <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> and the governing <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> said that, if elected, they would legislate on the issue. Labour said that it would introduce mandatory filters based on BBFC ratings if it believed that voluntary filtering by ISPs had failed.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The Conservatives said that they would give an independent regulator such as <a href="/wiki/ATVOD" title="ATVOD">ATVOD</a> the legal power to compel internet service providers to block sites which failed to include effective age verification.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>Proposals to create a single digital market for <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) member states include rules for <a href="/wiki/Net_neutrality" title="Net neutrality">net neutrality</a>. These rules require that all internet traffic has to be treated equally, without blocking or slowing down certain data. Net neutrality guidelines were announced in August 2016 by the <a href="/wiki/Body_of_European_Regulators_of_Electronic_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Body of European Regulators of Electronic Communications">Body of European Regulators of Electronic Communications</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> It is thought that the rules may restrict the legality of ISP filtering after 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In May 2014 the government suggested it would veto European net neutrality legislation due to its conflict with web blocking programmes.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> In May 2015, a leaked <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">Council of the European Union</a> document on the topic of net neutrality suggested users would have to opt into blocks, rather than opt out as per the current UK government's plans. John Carr of the UK Council for Child Internet Safety said of the proposals: 'a major plank of the UK’s approach to online child protection will be destroyed at a stroke'.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> However, the requirement that a UK government adheres to EU rules on net neutrality may disappear at some point in future when the <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">United Kingdom leaves the European Union</a>.</p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overblocking">Overblocking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Overblocking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>Wide-scale inadvertent 'overblocking' has been observed since ISP default filtering was introduced at the end of 2013. Legitimate sites are regularly blocked by the filters of some UK ISPs and mobile operators.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In December 2013 the UK Council for Child Internet Safety met with ISPs, charities, representatives from government, the BBFC and <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone_operator" title="Mobile phone operator">mobile phone operators</a> to seek ways to reduce the blocking of educational advice for young people. In January 2014 UKCCIS began constructing a whitelist of the charity-run educational sites for children that had been overblocked. The intention was to provide the list to ISPs to allow unblocking.<sup id="cite_ref-whitelist_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitelist-1">[1]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>Examples of overblocked categories reported include:<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p> 
      <ul> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a> and advice on <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">sexual health</a></li> 
       <li>help with <a href="/wiki/Sexual_addiction" title="Sexual addiction">sex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pornography_addiction" title="Pornography addiction">pornography addiction</a></li> 
       <li>support services for <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic abuse</a></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Child_protection" title="Child protection">child protection</a> services<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Suicide_prevention" title="Suicide prevention">suicide prevention</a></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Libraries" class="mw-redirect" title="Libraries">libraries</a></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliament</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> and <a href="/wiki/Politicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Politicians">politicians</a><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">drug</a> advice</li> 
      </ul> 
      <p>The identification of overblocked sites is made particularly difficult by the fact that ISPs do not provide checking tools to allow website owners to determine whether their site is being blocked.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In July 2014 the Open Rights Group launched an independent checking tool <i>blocked.org.uk</i>, a revamp of their mobile blocking site to report details of blocking on different fixed line ISPs and mobile providers. The tool revealed that 19% of 100,000 popularly visited websites were being blocked (with significant variation between ISPs) although the percentage of sites hosting legal pornographic material is thought to be around 4%.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>Significant underblocking has also been discovered, with ISPs failing to block up to 7% of adult sites tested.<sup id="cite_ref-Indy_Abuse_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indy_Abuse-87">[87]</a></sup> A study commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>'s Safer Internet Programme which tested parental control tools showed that underblocking for adult content ranged from 5-35%.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <h5><span class="mw-headline" id="In_favour">In favour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: In favour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5> 
      <p>Proponents of internet filtering are in favour of it primarily to combat the early sexualisation of children. The government believes that "broadband providers should consider automatically blocking sex sites, with individuals being required to opt in to receive them, rather than opt out and use the available computer parental controls."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In 2010 <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Culture,_Communications_and_Creative_Industries" class="mw-redirect" title="Minister for Culture, Communications and Creative Industries">communications minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Ed_Vaizey" title="Ed Vaizey">Ed Vaizey</a> was quoted as saying, "This is a very serious matter. I think it is very important that it's the ISPs that come up with solutions to protect children."</p> 
      <h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Against">Against</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Against">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5> 
      <p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i> described the UK's ISP filtering systems as creating "some of the strictest curbs on pornography in the Western world".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> There is no public scrutiny of the filtering lists. This creates the potential for them to be expanded to stifle dissent for political ends, as has happened in some other countries. Cameron has insisted that Internet users will have the option to turn the filters off, but no legislation exists to ensure that option will remain available.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>In March 2014, president <a href="/wiki/Diane_Duke" class="mw-redirect" title="Diane Duke">Diane Duke</a> of the United States-based <a href="/wiki/Free_Speech_Coalition" title="Free Speech Coalition">Free Speech Coalition</a> argued against the censorship rules at a <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> conference sponsored by Virgin Media. The discussion was titled "Switched on Families: Does the Online World Make Good Things Happen?". The panel included government representatives such as <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">Member of Parliament</a> Claire Perry, members of the press, and supporters of an open Internet such representatives from the UK Council for Child Internet Safety, the <a href="/wiki/Family_Online_Safety_Institute" title="Family Online Safety Institute">Family Online Safety Institute</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Big_Brother_Watch" title="Big Brother Watch">Big Brother Watch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2014AVNVirginFSP_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014AVNVirginFSP-93">[93]</a></sup> A report on the meeting was printed in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> on 5 March 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-GuardianVirginpanel_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GuardianVirginpanel-94">[94]</a></sup> Duke was quoted as saying, "The filters Prime Minister Cameron supports block <a href="/wiki/Sexual_health" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexual health">sexual health</a> sites, they block domestic violence sites, they block <a href="/wiki/Gay" title="Gay">gay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lesbian" title="Lesbian">lesbian</a> sites, they block information about <a href="/wiki/Eating_disorder" title="Eating disorder">eating disorders</a> and a lot of information to which it's crucial young people have access. Rather than protect children from things like <a href="/wiki/Bullying" title="Bullying">bullying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Online_predator" title="Online predator">online predators</a>, these filters leave children in the dark."</p> 
      <p>The Open Rights Group has been highly critical of the blocking programmes, especially mobile blocking and ISP default blocking. <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i> magazine observed that overblocking means “the most vulnerable people in society are the most likely to be cut off from the help they need”.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Categories_blocked">Categories blocked</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Categories blocked">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In July 2013 the Open Rights Group discovered from the ISPs that a wide range of content categories would be blocked.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Blocking has subsequently been detected in all the categories listed by the ISPs apart from '<a href="/wiki/Anorexia_nervosa" title="Anorexia nervosa">anorexia</a> and eating disorder websites' and '<a href="/wiki/Esotericism" class="mw-redirect" title="Esotericism">esoteric material</a>'. More information was gained following the launch of <i>blocked.org.uk</i> by the Open Rights Group, when TalkTalk gave additional detail about their default blocked categories and BT identified their default filtering level (light).<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobile_Internet_blocking">Mobile Internet blocking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mobile Internet blocking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>UK mobile phone operators began filtering Internet content in 2004<sup id="cite_ref-openrightsgroup.org_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openrightsgroup.org-9">[9]</a></sup> when Ofcom published a "UK code of practice for the self-regulation of new forms of content on mobiles".<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> This provided a means of classifying mobile Internet content to enable consistency in filtering. All major UK operators now voluntarily filter content by default. Nevertheless, in October 2014 it was reported that Ministers were drafting legislation to compel mobile operators to block access to adult sites unless users prove they are aged 18 or over.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>When users try to access blocked content they are redirected to a warning page. This tells them that they are not able to access an 'over 18 status' Internet site and a filtering mechanism has restricted their access. Categories that are listed as blocked include: adult / sexually explicit, chat, criminal skills, <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drugs</a>, alcohol and tobacco, gambling, <a href="/wiki/Computer_crime" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer crime">hacking</a>, hate, personal and dating, violence, and weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-bt_blocked_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bt_blocked-111">[111]</a></sup> Users who are adults may have the block lifted on request.<sup id="cite_ref-bt_blocked_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bt_blocked-111">[111]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>Guidelines published by the <i>Independent Mobile Classification Body</i> were used by mobile operators to classify sites until the British Board of Film Classification took over responsibility in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Classification determines whether content is suitable for customers under 18 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-bbfcframwork_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbfcframwork-113">[113]</a></sup> The default assumption is that a user is under 18.</p> 
      <p>The following content types must be blocked from under 18's:<sup id="cite_ref-bbfcframwork_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbfcframwork-113">[113]</a></sup></p> 
      <ul> 
       <li>Suicide, Self-harm, <a href="/wiki/Pro-Anorexia" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro-Anorexia">Pro-Anorexia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eating_disorders" class="mw-redirect" title="Eating disorders">eating disorders</a></li> 
       <li>Discriminatory language</li> 
       <li>Encouragement of Drug Use</li> 
       <li>Repeated / aggressive use of '<a href="/wiki/Cunt" title="Cunt">cunt</a>'</li> 
       <li>Pornography Restrictions</li> 
       <li>Violence and Gore restrictions</li> 
      </ul> 
      <p>Significant overblocking of Internet sites by mobile operators is reported, including the blocking of <a href="/wiki/Political_satire" title="Political satire">political satire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a> and gay content.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Research by the <i>Open Rights Group</i> highlighted the widespread nature of unjustified site blocking.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In 2011 the group set up Blocked.org.uk, a website allowing the reporting of sites and services that are 'blocked' on their mobile network.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The website received hundreds of reports<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> of the blocking of sites covering <a href="/wiki/Blog" title="Blog">blogs</a>, business, <a href="/wiki/Internet_privacy" title="Internet privacy">internet privacy</a> and internet forums across multiple networks. The <i>Open Rights Group</i> also demonstrated that correcting the erroneous blocking of innocent sites can be difficult. No UK mobile operator provides an on-line tool for identifying blocked websites. The <i>O2 Website status checker</i><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> was available until the end of 2013 but was suspended in December<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> after it had been widely used to determine the extent of overblocking by O2.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Not only were <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computing" title="Computing">computing</a> sites being blocked,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> but also <a href="/wiki/Childline" title="Childline">Childline</a>, the <a href="/wiki/NSPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="NSPCC">NSPCC</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Police" title="Police">Police</a>. An additional opt-in whitelist service aimed at users under 12 years is provided by O2. The service only allows access to websites on a list of categories deemed suitable for that age group.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet_Watch_Foundation">Internet Watch Foundation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Internet Watch Foundation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse_image_content_list" title="Child abuse image content list">Child abuse image content list</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Between 2004 and 2006, BT Group introduced its Cleanfeed <a href="/wiki/Content_blocking_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Content blocking system">content blocking system</a> technology<sup id="cite_ref-theyworkforyou.com_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theyworkforyou.com-125">[125]</a></sup> to implement 'section 97A'<sup id="cite_ref-richemont_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richemont-126">[126]</a></sup> orders. BT spokesman Jon Carter described Cleanfeed's function as "to block access to illegal Web sites that are listed by the Internet Watch Foundation", and described it as essentially a server hosting a filter that checked requested URLs for Web sites on the IWF list, and returning an error message of "Web site not found" for positive matches.<sup id="cite_ref-BT_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BT-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arnfield2004_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arnfield2004-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Cleanfeed is a silent content filtering system, which means that Internet users cannot ascertain whether they are being regulated by Cleanfeed, experiencing connection failures, or if the page really does not exist. The proportion of Internet Service Providers using Cleanfeed by the beginning of 2006 was 80%<sup id="cite_ref-theyworkforyou.com_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theyworkforyou.com-125">[125]</a></sup> and this rose to 95% by the middle of 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> In February 2009, the Government said that it was looking at ways to cover the final 5%.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2009a_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2009a-131">[131]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>According to a small-sample survey conducted in 2008 by Nikolaos Koumartzis, an MA researcher at London College of Communication, the vast majority of UK based Internet users (90.21%) were unaware of the existence of Cleanfeed software. Moreover, nearly two thirds of the participants did not trust British Telecommunications or the IWF to be responsible for a silent censorship system in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> A majority would prefer to see a message stating that a given site was blocked and to have access to a form for unblocking a given site.</p> 
      <p>Cleanfeed originally targeted only alleged child sexual abuse content identified by the Internet Watch Foundation. However, no safeguards exist to stop the secret list of blocked sites being extended to include sites unrelated to <a href="/wiki/Child_pornography" title="Child pornography">child pornography</a>. This had led to criticism of Cleanfeed's lack of transparency which gives it considerable potential for broad censorship. Further, Cleanfeed has been used to block access to copyright-infringing websites after a court order in 2011 required BT to block access to NewzBin2.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCcopyright_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCcopyright-30">[30]</a></sup> This has led some to describe Cleanfeed as the most perfectly invisible censorship mechanism ever invented and to liken its powers of censorship to those employed currently by China.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> There are risks that increasing Internet regulation will lead the Internet to be even more restricted in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>Non-BT ISPs now implement the child abuse image content list with their in-house technologies to implement IWF blocking.</p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="IWF.2FWikipedia_controversy">IWF/Wikipedia controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: IWF/Wikipedia controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Internet_Watch_Foundation_and_Wikipedia" title="Internet Watch Foundation and Wikipedia">Internet Watch Foundation and Wikipedia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On 5 December 2008 the IWF system blacklisted a Wikipedia article on the <a href="/wiki/Scorpions_(Band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Scorpions (Band)">Scorpions</a> album <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Killer" title="Virgin Killer">Virgin Killer</a>. A statement by the organisation's spokesman alleged that the album cover, displayed in the article, contained "a potentially illegal indecent image of a child under the age of 18".<sup id="cite_ref-WP_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP-136">[136]</a></sup> Users of major ISPs, including Virgin Media, Be/O2/Telefónica, EasyNet/UK Online, Demon and Opal, were unable to access the content, despite the album cover being available unfiltered on other major sites including <a href="/wiki/Amazon.co.uk" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon.co.uk">Amazon.co.uk</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-WP_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP-136">[136]</a></sup> and available for sale in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson2008_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson2008-137">[137]</a></sup> The system also started proxying users, who accessed any Wikipedia article, via a minimal number of servers, which resulted in site administrators having to block them from editing Wikipedia or creating accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008_register_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008_register-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008_zdnet_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008_zdnet-139">[139]</a></sup> On 9 December, the IWF removed the article from its blacklist, stating: "IWF's overriding objective is to minimise the availability of indecent images of children on the Internet, however, on this occasion our efforts have had the opposite effect."<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008_bbc_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008_bbc-140">[140]</a></sup></p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_Wi-Fi">Public Wi-Fi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Public Wi-Fi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The vast majority of the Internet access provided by <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> systems in public places in the UK is filtered with many sites being blocked. The filtering is done voluntarily by the six largest providers of public Wi-Fi: Arqiva, BT, Sky, Nomad Digital, Virgin and O2, who together are responsible for 90% public Wi-Fi.<sup id="cite_ref-ITV_News_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITV_News-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mail_Blocks_on_Internet_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mail_Blocks_on_Internet-60">[60]</a></sup> The filtering was introduced as a result of an agreement put in place in November 2013 between the Government and the Wi-Fi providers. Pressure from the Government and the UK Council for Child Internet Safety<sup id="cite_ref-gov.uk_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gov.uk-48">[48]</a></sup> had already led Virgin and O2 to install filtering on the Wi-Fi systems on the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a> restaurants,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> but half of all public Wi-Fi networks remained unfiltered in September 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-publicwificoverage_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-publicwificoverage-11">[11]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>"Overblocking" is a problem reported with public Wi-Fi filters. Research in September 2013 indicated that poorly programmed filters blocked sites when a prohibited tag appeared coincidentally within an unrelated word. Religious sites were blocked by nearly half of public Wi-Fi filters and sex education sites were blocked by one third.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> In November 2013, there were complaints about the blocking of Gay websites that were not related to sex or nudity on the public Wi-Fi provided by <a href="/wiki/Train_operating_company" title="Train operating company">train operating companies</a>. The filtering was done by third party organisations and these were criticised for being both unidentified and unaccountable. Such blocking may breach the <a href="/wiki/Equality_Act_2010" title="Equality Act 2010">Equality Act 2010</a>. The government arranged for the UK Council for Child Internet Safety to investigate whether filters were blocking advice to young people in areas such as sex education.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libraries_and_educational_institutions">Libraries and educational institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Libraries and educational institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Many libraries in the UK such as the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-145">[145]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Local_authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Local authority">local authority</a> <a href="/wiki/Public_libraries" class="mw-redirect" title="Public libraries">public libraries</a><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> apply filters to Internet access. According to research conducted by the Radical Librarians Collective, at least 98% of public libraries apply filters; including categories such as "LGBT interest", "abortion" and "questionable".<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Some public libraries block <a href="/wiki/Payday_loan" title="Payday loan">Payday loan</a> websites<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lambeth_London_Borough_Council" title="Lambeth London Borough Council">Lambeth Council</a> has called for other public Wi-fi providers to block these sites too.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>The majority of schools and colleges use filters to block access to sites which contain adult material, gambling and sites which contain malware. <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>, <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> are often filtered by schools. Some universities also block access to sites containing a variety of material.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Many students often use proxy servers to bypass this.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Newsbeat_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Newsbeat-151">[151]</a></sup> Schools often censor pupils' Internet access in order to offer some protection against various perceived threats such as cyber-bullying and the perceived risk of grooming by paedophiles; as well as to maintain pupil attention during IT lessons. Examples of overblocking exist in the school context. For instance, in February 2014 the website of the <a href="/wiki/Yes_Scotland" title="Yes Scotland">Yes Scotland</a> pro-independence campaign was blocked in a Glasgow school while the rival <a href="/wiki/Better_Together_(campaign)" title="Better Together (campaign)">Better Together</a> pro-union website was not blocked.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planned_programmes">Planned programmes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Planned programmes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extremism">Extremism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Extremism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For more details on this topic, see 
       <a href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_the_United_Kingdom#Politics_and_extremism" title="Internet censorship in the United Kingdom">Internet censorship in the United Kingdom §&nbsp;Politics and extremism</a>.
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Counter_Terrorism_Internet_Referral_Unit" title="Counter Terrorism Internet Referral Unit">Counter Terrorism Internet Referral Unit</a> (CTIRU), which was set up in 2010 by the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Chief_Police_Officers" title="Association of Chief Police Officers">Association of Chief Police Officers</a> and run by the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police Service</a>, maintains a list of sites and content that in their opinion incites or glorifies terrorist acts under Section 3 of the <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_Act_2006" title="Terrorism Act 2006">Terrorism Act 2006</a>. This list is passed to the public estate institutions so that access to the sites can be blocked. ISPs BT, Sky, TalkTalk and Virgin Media incorporate the CTIRU block list into their filters.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> CTIRU also issues removal requests if the Internet content hosted in the UK .<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The UK is the only country in the world with such a unit.</p> 
      <p>Home Office proposals in 2006 requiring ISPs to block access to articles "glorifying terrorism"<sup id="cite_ref-LINX_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LINX-155">[155]</a></sup> were rejected and the government opted for a takedown approach at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-ORG_Counter_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ORG_Counter-156">[156]</a></sup> However, in December 2013 the Prime Minister's Extremism task force proposed that where such material is hosted overseas, ISPs should block the websites,<sup id="cite_ref-ORG_Counter_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ORG_Counter-156">[156]</a></sup> and David Cameron gave orders that the CTIRU list be extended to UK ISPs.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The UK government has defined extremism as: "Vocal or active opposition to fundamental British values, including democracy, the rule of law, individual liberty and mutual respect and tolerance of different faiths and beliefs."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>This approach to web blocking has been criticised for being extra-parliamentary and extrajudicial<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> and for being a proactive process where authorities actively seek out material to ban.<sup id="cite_ref-terroristandextremist_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-terroristandextremist-14">[14]</a></sup> Additionally, concerns have been expressed by ISPs and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a> advocates that these measures could lead to the censorship of content that is “extremist” but not illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-Indy_Abuse_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indy_Abuse-87">[87]</a></sup> Indeed, the United Kingdom security minister <a href="/wiki/James_Brokenshire" title="James Brokenshire">James Brokenshire</a> said in March 2014 that the government should also deal with material "that may not be illegal but certainly is unsavoury and may not be the sort of material that people would want to see or receive".<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Unimplemented_proposals">Unimplemented proposals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Unimplemented proposals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_media_and_communications">Social media and communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Social media and communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>On 11 August 2011, following the widespread <a href="/wiki/2011_England_riots" title="2011 England riots">riots in England</a>, British Prime Minister David Cameron said that Theresa May, the Home secretary, would meet with executives of the Web companies Facebook and Twitter, as well as <a href="/wiki/BlackBerry_Limited" title="BlackBerry Limited">Research In Motion</a>, maker of the <a href="/wiki/BlackBerry" title="BlackBerry">BlackBerry</a> smartphone, to discuss possible measures to prevent troublemakers from using <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a> and other digital communications tools.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> During a special debate on the riots, Mr. Cameron told Parliament:</p> 
      <blockquote> 
       <p>Everyone watching these horrific actions will be struck by how they were organized via social media. Free flow of information can be used for good. But it can also be used for ill. And when people are using social media for violence we need to stop them. So we are working with the police, the intelligence services and industry to look at whether it would be right to stop people communicating via these Web sites and services when we know they are plotting violence, disorder and criminality”.</p> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Critics say that the British government is considering policies similar to those it has criticized in totalitarian and one-party states.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> And in the immediate aftermath of the riots, Iran, often criticized by the West for restricting the Internet and curbing free speech, offered to "send a human rights delegation to Britain to study human rights violations in the country".<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>On 25 August 2011 British officials and representatives of Twitter, Facebook and BlackBerry met privately to discuss voluntary ways to limit or restrict the use of social media to combat crime and periods of civil unrest.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The government is seeking ways to crack down on networks being used for criminal behavior, but is not seeking any additional powers and has no intention of restricting Internet services.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> It was not clear what new measures, if any, would be taken as a result of the meeting.</p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_members.27_bills">Private members' bills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Private members' bills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/Private_Members%27_Bills_in_the_Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Private Members' Bills in the Parliament of the United Kingdom">private members bill</a> requiring ISPs, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone_operators" class="mw-redirect" title="Mobile phone operators">mobile phone operators</a> and equipment manufacturers to filter adult content was introduced into the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> in May 2012 by <a href="/wiki/Elspeth_Howe,_Baroness_Howe_of_Idlicote" title="Elspeth Howe, Baroness Howe of Idlicote">Baroness Howe of Idlicote</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> The <i>Online Safety Bill</i> was criticised for its potential to block any service that appears to provide adult material unless it is on an Ofcom-approved list.<sup id="cite_ref-Sex_Alert_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sex_Alert-167">[167]</a></sup> The original bill did not succeed due to a lack of Government support.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> It was re-introduced in May 2015 and failed a second time.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>In September 2014 as a proposed addition to UK legislation against <a href="/wiki/Revenge_porn" title="Revenge porn">revenge porn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geraint_Davies_(Labour_politician)" title="Geraint Davies (Labour politician)">Geraint Davies</a> MP introduced a private member's bill mandating devices that can access the Internet be filtered by default at the threat of fining non-compliant manufacturers.</p> 
      <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
       <p>whereby if mobile phones, computers and other devices that have access to the internet are not sold in a default position without that access—that is, if the user has to switch it on or contact the supplier—we could fine the manufacturers<sup id="cite_ref-survivetheclaireperryinter1_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-survivetheclaireperryinter1-18">[18]</a></sup></p> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>After the bill's first reading there was no debate and the bill made no further progress.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p> 
      <p>Although these legislative approaches were unsuccessful as private member' bills, their measures may appear in a future Government Communications Bill.<sup id="cite_ref-Sex_Alert_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sex_Alert-167">[167]</a></sup></p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technologies">Technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_ISP">By ISP</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: By ISP">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>A service provider will integrate some or all its feeds into a single filtering device or stack, sometimes in conjunction with an <a href="/wiki/Upstream_(networking)" title="Upstream (networking)">upstream</a> provider performing additional filtering. The following content-control technologies have been confirmed to be used to implement all types of web blocking (includes <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_Kingdom_mobile_virtual_network_operators" title="List of United Kingdom mobile virtual network operators">virtual operators</a>):</p>

      </table> 
      <p>Rulespace and O2 are the only known services with a public categorisation and blocking check tool.</p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_feed_type">By feed type</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: By feed type">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <table class="wikitable sortable"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th>Programme</th> 
         <th>Implementation</th> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">Corporate</a> filtering (most possible)</td> 
         <td><a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">SSL</a> enabled <a href="/wiki/Deep_packet_inspection" title="Deep packet inspection">deep packet inspection</a> with <a href="/wiki/Uniform_resource_identifier" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform resource identifier">URI</a> blocking<br> <a href="/wiki/IP_address_blocking" title="IP address blocking">IP address blocking</a><br> <a href="/wiki/DNS_hijacking" title="DNS hijacking">DNS hijacking</a><br></td> 
        </tr>

         <td><a href="/wiki/Deep_packet_inspection" title="Deep packet inspection">Deep packet inspection</a> with <a href="/wiki/Uniform_resource_identifier" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform resource identifier">URI</a> Blocking<br> <a href="/wiki/IP_address_blocking" title="IP address blocking">IP address blocking</a><br> <a href="/wiki/DNS_hijacking" title="DNS hijacking">DNS hijacking</a></td>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Circumvention">Circumvention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Circumvention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_circumvention" title="Internet censorship circumvention">Internet censorship circumvention</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Site blocks can be circumvented using method trivial through to complex such as use of Tor, <a href="/wiki/Virtual_private_network" title="Virtual private network">VPNs</a>, site specific and general web proxies,<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> and other circumvention techniques.</p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_abuse_image_content_list">Child abuse image content list</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Child abuse image content list">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Due to the proxy server implementation of the IWF's child abuse image content list (formally Cleanfeed) system, websites that <a href="/wiki/Ingress_filtering" title="Ingress filtering">filter users by IP address</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Wiki" title="Wiki">wikis</a> and <a href="/wiki/File_hosting_service" title="File hosting service">file lockers</a>, will be significantly broken,<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> even if only a tiny proportion of its content is flagged.</p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Copyright_2">Copyright</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Copyright">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In response to the increasing number of file sharing related blocks, a number of proxy aggregator sites, e.g. torrentproxies.com, have become popular.<sup id="cite_ref-proxyblocks_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-proxyblocks-189">[189]</a></sup> In addition to the following, proxy sites designed to circumvent blocks have been secretly blocked by ISPs, driving users to proxy comparison sites.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pirate_Bay" class="mw-redirect" title="Pirate Bay">Pirate Bay</a> created a version of Tor branded as the <a href="/wiki/PirateBrowser" title="PirateBrowser">PirateBrowser</a> specifically to encourage anonymity and circumvention of these blocks.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> On August 5, 2014, City of London <a href="/wiki/Police_Intellectual_Property_Crime_Unit" title="Police Intellectual Property Crime Unit">Police Intellectual Property Crime Unit</a> arrested a 20-year-old man in Nottingham on suspicion of operating a proxy server that allowed internet users to bypass blocks on many popular sites.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ISP_default_network_blocking_2">ISP default network blocking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: ISP default network blocking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Downloadable software enabling <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browsers</a> to bypass the ISP filtering began appearing in December 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> and in 2014 versions began appearing for mobile Internet platforms.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_blocking_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <ul> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Internet_censorship" title="Internet censorship">Internet censorship</a></li>

      </ul>
